<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Mesoamerica</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the cultural area. For the economic region, see <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_region" title="Mesoamerican region">Mesoamerican region</a>. For the geographic area, see <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mesoamerica_(disambiguation)" title="Mesoamerica (disambiguation)">Mesoamerica (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Pre-Columbian cultural area in the Americas</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mesoamerica_english.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mesoamerica_english.PNG/330px-Mesoamerica_english.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mesoamerica_english.PNG/495px-Mesoamerica_english.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mesoamerica_english.PNG/660px-Mesoamerica_english.PNG 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mesoamerica_english.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mesoamerica and its cultural areas<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2021)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Mesoamerica</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Historical_region" title="Historical region">historical region</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_area" title="Cultural area">cultural area</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and most of <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>. It extends from approximately central <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> through <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, and northern <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>. Within this region <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian societies</a> flourished for more than 3,000 years before the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonization of the Americas</a>. Mesoamerica was the site of two of the most profound historical transformations in world history: primary urban generation, and the formation of <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> cultures out of the long encounters among indigenous, European, African and Asian cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th century, Eurasian diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, which were endemic among the colonists but new to North America, caused the deaths of upwards of 90% of the indigenous people, resulting in great losses to their societies and cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Mesoamerica is one of the five areas in the world where ancient <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> arose independently (see <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">cradle of civilization</a>), and the second in the Americas. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norte_Chico_civilization" title="Norte Chico civilization">Norte Chico</a> (Caral-Supe) in present-day <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, arose as an independent civilization in the northern coastal region.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg/220px-Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg/330px-Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg/440px-Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monte_Alb%C3%A1n-12-05oaxaca024.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ballgame court at <a href="/wiki/Monte_Alb%C3%A1n" title="Monte Albán">Monte Albán</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg/220px-Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg/330px-Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg/440px-Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columpio_Veracruz_059.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of swinging <a href="/wiki/Remojadas" title="Remojadas">Remojadas</a> figurines, <a href="/wiki/Classic_Veracruz_culture" title="Classic Veracruz culture">Classic Veracruz culture</a>, 300 to 900 CE.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3332" data-file-width="1552" decoding="async" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg/170px-Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg/255px-Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg/340px-Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dresden_Codex_p09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page 9 of the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Codex" title="Dresden Codex">Dresden Codex</a> (from the 1880 Förstermann edition)</div></div></div>
<p>As a cultural area, Mesoamerica is defined by a mosaic of cultural traits developed and shared by its indigenous cultures. Beginning as early as 7000 BCE, the domestication of <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">cacao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaseolus" title="Phaseolus">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meleagris_gallopavo" title="Meleagris gallopavo">turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xoloitzcuintle" title="Xoloitzcuintle">dog</a>, resulted in a transition from <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">paleo-Indian</a> hunter-gatherer tribal groupings to the organization of sedentary agricultural villages. In the subsequent Formative period, agriculture and cultural traits such as a complex <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_religion" title="Mesoamerican religion">mythological and religious tradition</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">vigesimal</a> numeric system, a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_calendars" title="Mesoamerican calendars">complex calendric system</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballgame" title="Mesoamerican ballgame">tradition of ball playing</a>, and a distinct <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_architecture" title="Mesoamerican architecture">architectural style</a>, were diffused through the area. Also in this period, villages began to become socially stratified and develop into <a href="/wiki/Chiefdom" title="Chiefdom">chiefdoms</a>. Large ceremonial centers were built, interconnected by a network of trade routes for the exchange of luxury goods, such as <a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">obsidian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cacao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinnabar" title="Cinnabar">cinnabar</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Spondylus" title="Spondylus">Spondylus</a></i> shells, <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a>, and ceramics. While Mesoamerican civilization knew of the <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a> and basic <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, neither of these became technologically relevant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen199655_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen199655-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the earliest complex civilizations was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a> culture, which inhabited the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_Mexico" title="Gulf Coast of Mexico">Gulf Coast of Mexico</a> and extended inland and southwards across the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Tehuantepec" title="Isthmus of Tehuantepec">Isthmus of Tehuantepec</a>. Frequent contact and cultural interchange between the early Olmec and other cultures in Chiapas, Oaxaca and Guatemala laid the basis for the Mesoamerican cultural area. All this was facilitated by considerable <a href="/wiki/Regional_communications_in_ancient_Mesoamerica" title="Regional communications in ancient Mesoamerica">regional communications in ancient Mesoamerica</a>, especially along the Pacific coast.
</p><p>During this formative period distinct religious and symbolic traditions spread, as well as the development of artistic and architectural complexes. In the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Preclassic period</a>, complex urban polities began to develop among the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a>, with the rise of centers such as <a href="/wiki/Aguada_F%C3%A9nix" title="Aguada Fénix">Aguada fénix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calakmul" title="Calakmul">Calakmul</a> in Mexico; <a href="/wiki/El_Mirador" title="El Mirador">El Mirador</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a> in Guatemala, and the <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilization" title="Zapotec civilization">Zapotec</a> at <a href="/wiki/Monte_Alb%C3%A1n" title="Monte Albán">Monte Albán</a>. During this period, the first true <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_writing_systems" title="Mesoamerican writing systems">Mesoamerican writing systems</a> were developed in the <a href="/wiki/Epi-Olmec_culture" title="Epi-Olmec culture">Epi-Olmec</a> and the Zapotec cultures. The Mesoamerican writing tradition reached its height in the Classic <a href="/wiki/Maya_script" title="Maya script">Maya logosyllabic script</a>.
</p><p>Mesoamerica is one of only six regions of the world where writing is known to have independently developed (the others being ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andean_civilizations" title="Andean civilizations">Peru</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">China</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaganBeck1996_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FaganBeck1996-5">[5]</a></sup> In Central Mexico, the city of <a href="/wiki/Teotihuacan" title="Teotihuacan">Teotihuacan</a> ascended at the height of the Classic period; it formed a military and commercial empire whose political influence stretched south into the Maya area and northward. Upon the collapse of Teotihuacán around 600 AD, competition between several important political centers in central Mexico, such as <a href="/wiki/Xochicalco" title="Xochicalco">Xochicalco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cholula_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Cholula (Mesoamerican site)">Cholula</a>, ensued. At this time during the Epi-Classic period, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahua_people" title="Nahua people">Nahua peoples</a> began moving south into Mesoamerica from the North, and became politically and culturally dominant in central Mexico, as they displaced speakers of <a href="/wiki/Oto-Manguean_languages" title="Oto-Manguean languages">Oto-Manguean languages</a>.
</p><p>During the early post-Classic period, Central Mexico was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Toltec" title="Toltec">Toltec</a> culture, and Oaxaca by the <a href="/wiki/Mixtec" title="Mixtec">Mixtec</a>. The lowland Maya area had important centers at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1" title="Chichén Itzá">Chichén Itzá</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayap%C3%A1n" title="Mayapán">Mayapán</a>. Towards the end of the post-Classic period, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> of Central Mexico built a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tributary_states" title="Tributary states">tributary</a> empire covering most of central Mesoamerica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen199640–80_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen199640–80-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The distinct Mesoamerican cultural tradition ended with the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Aztec_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire">Spanish conquest</a> in the 16th century. Over the next centuries, Mesoamerican indigenous cultures were gradually subjected to Spanish colonial rule. Aspects of the Mesoamerican cultural heritage still survive among the indigenous peoples who inhabit Mesoamerica. Many continue to speak their ancestral languages, and maintain many practices harking back to their Mesoamerican roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen1996_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen1996-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_definition">Etymology and definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:-moz-fit-content; width:-webkit-fit-content; width:fit-content;"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:auto;">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 75px"><div style="width: 75px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 70px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg" title="Cihuatán"><img alt="Cihuatán" data-file-height="2515" data-file-width="7392" decoding="async" height="24" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg/70px-Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg/105px-Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg/140px-Piramide_Cihuatan.jpg 2x" width="70"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>Mesoamerica</i> literally means "middle America" in Greek. <a href="/wiki/Middle_America_(Americas)" title="Middle America (Americas)">Middle America</a> often refers to a larger area in the Americas, but it has also previously been used more narrowly to refer to Mesoamerica. An example is the title of the 16 volumes of <i>The Handbook of Middle American Indians</i>.  "Mesoamerica" is broadly defined as the area that is home to the Mesoamerican civilization, which comprises a group of peoples with close cultural and historical ties. The exact geographic extent of Mesoamerica has varied through time, as the civilization extended North and South from its heartland in southern Mexico.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:164px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5152" data-file-width="3864" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg/162px-Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg/243px-Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg/324px-Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg 2x" width="162"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proto_Lencan_Statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Antropomorphic figure from the Proto-<a href="/wiki/Lenca" title="Lenca">Lencan</a> culture found at <a href="/wiki/Los_Naranjos,_Honduras" title="Los Naranjos, Honduras">Los Naranjos</a>, Honduras. An example of mesomerican art during the preclassic Period.</div></div></div>
<p>The term was first used by the <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethnology" title="Ethnology">ethnologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kirchhoff" title="Paul Kirchhoff">Paul Kirchhoff</a>, who noted that similarities existed among the various <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian cultures</a> within the region that included southern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> lowlands of <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>. In the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Culture-historical_archaeology" title="Culture-historical archaeology">cultural history</a>, the prevalent <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_theory" title="Archaeological theory">archaeological theory</a> of the early to middle 20th century, Kirchhoff defined this zone as a cultural area based on a suite of interrelated cultural similarities brought about by millennia of inter- and intra-regional interaction (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Trans-cultural_diffusion" title="Trans-cultural diffusion">diffusion</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirchhoff1943_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirchhoff1943-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen19965–8_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen19965–8-9">[9]</a></sup> Mesoamerica is recognized as a near-prototypical cultural area. This term is now fully integrated in the standard terminology of pre-Columbian <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">anthropological</a> studies. Conversely, the sister terms <a href="/wiki/Aridoamerica" title="Aridoamerica">Aridoamerica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oasisamerica" title="Oasisamerica">Oasisamerica</a>, which refer to northern Mexico and the western <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, respectively, have not entered into widespread usage.
</p><p>Some of the significant cultural traits defining the Mesoamerican cultural tradition are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Horticulture and plant use</i>: <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floating_gardens" title="Floating gardens">floating gardens</a>; use of <a href="/wiki/Amate" title="Amate">bark paper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agave" title="Agave">agave</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Maguey" title="Maguey">maguey</a>) for ritual purposes, as a medium for writing, and the use of agave for cooking and clothing; cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cacao</a>; grinding of corn softened with ashes or <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>; harpoon-shaped <a href="/wiki/Digging_stick" title="Digging stick">digging stick</a></li>
<li><i>Clothing and personal articles</i>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lip_plugs" title="Lip plugs">lip plugs</a>, mirrors of polished stone, turbans, sandals with heels, textiles adorned with <a href="/wiki/Rabbit_hair" title="Rabbit hair">rabbit hair</a></li>
<li><i>Architecture</i>: construction of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_pyramids" title="Mesoamerican pyramids">stepped pyramids</a>; <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a> floors; ball courts with stone rings (see the use of <a href="/wiki/Natural_rubber" title="Natural rubber">natural rubber</a> and the practice of the ritual <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballgame" title="Mesoamerican ballgame">Mesoamerican ballgame</a>)</li>
<li><i>Record keeping</i>: use of two different <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_calendars" title="Mesoamerican calendars">calendars</a> (a 260-day ritual calendar and a 365-day calendar based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_year" title="Solar year">solar year</a>); use of locally developed <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictographic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hieroglyph" title="Hieroglyph">hieroglyphic</a> (logo-syllabic) <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_writing_systems" title="Mesoamerican writing systems">writing systems</a>; numbers (see also <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">vigesimal</a> (base 20) number system); "century" of fifty-two years; eighteen-month calendar; screen-fold books</li>
<li><i>Commerce</i>: specialized markets, "department store" markets subdivided according to specialty</li>
<li><i>Weapons and warfare</i>: wooden swords with stone chips set into the edges (see <a href="/wiki/Macuahuitl" title="Macuahuitl">macuahuitl</a>), military orders (eagle knights and jaguar knights), clay pellets for blowguns, cotton-pad armor, traveling merchants who act as spies, wars for the purpose of securing sacrificial victims</li>
<li><i>Ritual and myth</i>: practice of various forms of ritual <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifice</a> and quail sacrifice; paper and rubber as sacrificial offerings; pantheon of gods or spirits; acrobatic flier dance (see the <a href="/wiki/Danza_de_los_Voladores" title="Danza de los Voladores">Danza de los Voladores</a> and the Totonac flier dance; 13 as a ritual number; ritual period of 20 x 13 = 260 days; mythic concept of one or more afterworlds and the difficult journey in reaching them; good and bad omen days; a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_religion" title="Mesoamerican religion">religious complex</a> based on a combination of <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a> and natural deities, and a shared system of symbols</li>
<li><i>Language</i>: a <a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">linguistic area</a> defined by a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_language_area" title="Mesoamerican language area">number of grammatical traits</a> that have spread through the area by diffusion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbellKaufmanSmith-Stark1986_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbellKaufmanSmith-Stark1986-11">[11]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mesoamerica" title="Geography of Mesoamerica">Geography of Mesoamerica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_archer10_(Dennis)_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_archer10_(Dennis)_-_Guatemala_1828_-_La_Danta_at_the_Mayan_site_of_El_Mirador.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/El_Mirador" title="El Mirador">El Mirador</a> flourished from 600 BCE to 100 CE, and may have had a population of over 100,000.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puebla_farmers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="951" data-file-width="1365" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Puebla_farmers.jpg/220px-Puebla_farmers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Puebla_farmers.jpg/330px-Puebla_farmers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Puebla_farmers.jpg/440px-Puebla_farmers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puebla_farmers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Landscape of the Mesoamerican highlands</div></div></div>
<p>Located on the <a href="/wiki/Middle_America_(Americas)" title="Middle America (Americas)">Middle American</a> <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmus</a> joining North and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> between <i>ca.</i> 10° and 22° northern <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, Mesoamerica possesses a complex combination of ecological systems, topographic zones, and environmental contexts. These different <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a> are classified into two broad categories: the lowlands (those areas between <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> and 1000 meters) and the <i>altiplanos</i>, or highlands (situated between 1,000 and 2,000 meters above sea level).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoe1994_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoe1994-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen19969–11_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarmackGascoGossen19969–11-13">[13]</a></sup> In the low-lying regions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_climate" title="Subtropical climate">sub-tropical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical climates</a> are most common, as is true for most of the coastline along the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>. The highlands show much more climatic diversity, ranging from dry tropical to cold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_climate" title="Mountain climate">mountainous climates</a>; the dominant climate is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperateness" title="Temperateness">temperate</a> with warm temperatures and moderate rainfall. The rainfall varies from the dry <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a> and north <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Yucatán">Yucatán</a> to the humid southern Pacific and Caribbean lowlands.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_sub-areas">Cultural sub-areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cultural sub-areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several distinct sub-regions within Mesoamerica are defined by a convergence of geographic and cultural attributes. These sub-regions are more conceptual than culturally meaningful, and the demarcation of their limits is not rigid. The Maya area, for example, can be divided into two general groups: the lowlands and highlands. The lowlands are further divided into the southern and northern Maya lowlands. The southern Maya lowlands are generally regarded as encompassing northern <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Campeche" title="Campeche">Campeche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quintana_Roo" title="Quintana Roo">Quintana Roo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>. The northern lowlands cover the remainder of the northern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>. Other areas include Central Mexico, West Mexico, the Gulf Coast Lowlands, <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a>, the Southern Pacific Lowlands, and Southeast Mesoamerica (including northern <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is extensive topographic variation in Mesoamerica, ranging from the high peaks circumscribing the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Mexico" title="Valley of Mexico">Valley of Mexico</a> and within the central <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Madre_de_Chiapas" title="Sierra Madre de Chiapas">Sierra Madre</a> mountains to the low flatlands of the northern Yucatán Peninsula. The tallest mountain in Mesoamerica is <a href="/wiki/Pico_de_Orizaba" title="Pico de Orizaba">Pico de Orizaba</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dormant_volcano" title="Dormant volcano">dormant volcano</a> located on the border of <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">Puebla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a>. Its peak elevation is 5,636 m (18,490 ft).
</p><p>The Sierra Madre mountains, which consist of several smaller ranges, run from northern Mesoamerica south through <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>. The chain is historically <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a>. In central and southern Mexico, a portion of the Sierra Madre chain is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eje_Volc%C3%A1nico_Transversal" title="Eje Volcánico Transversal">Eje Volcánico Transversal</a>, or the Trans-Mexican volcanic belt. There are 83 inactive and active volcanoes within the Sierra Madre range, including 11 in Mexico, 37 in Guatemala, 23 in El Salvador, 25 in Nicaragua, and 3 in northwestern Costa Rica. According to the Michigan Technological University,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> 16 of these are still active. The tallest active volcano is <a href="/wiki/Popocat%C3%A9petl" title="Popocatépetl">Popocatépetl</a> at 5,452 m (17,887 ft). This volcano, which retains its <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> name, is located 70 km (43 mi) southeast of <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>. Other volcanoes of note include Tacana on the Mexico–Guatemala border, <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_Tajumulco" title="Volcán Tajumulco">Tajumulco</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santamar%C3%ADa_(volcano)" title="Santamaría (volcano)">Santamaría</a> in Guatemala, <a href="/wiki/Izalco_(volcano)" title="Izalco (volcano)">Izalco</a> in El Salvador, <a href="/wiki/Arenal_Volcano" title="Arenal Volcano">Arenal</a> in Costa Rica, and <a href="/wiki/Concepci%C3%B3n_(volcano)" title="Concepción (volcano)">Concepción</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maderas" title="Maderas">Maderas</a> on <a href="/wiki/Ometepe" title="Ometepe">Ometepe</a>, which is an island formed by both volcanoes rising out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Cocibolca" title="Lake Cocibolca">Lake Cocibolca</a> in Nicaragua.
</p><p>One important topographic feature is the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Tehuantepec" title="Isthmus of Tehuantepec">Isthmus of Tehuantepec</a>, a low plateau that breaks up the Sierra Madre chain between the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Madre_del_Sur" title="Sierra Madre del Sur">Sierra Madre del Sur</a> to the north and the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Madre_de_Chiapas" title="Sierra Madre de Chiapas">Sierra Madre de Chiapas</a> to the south. At its highest point, the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">Isthmus</a> is 224 m (735 ft) above mean sea level. This area also represents the shortest distance between the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> in Mexico. The distance between the two coasts is roughly 200 km (120 mi). The northern side of the Isthmus is swampy and covered in dense jungle—but the Isthmus of Tehuantepec, as the lowest and most level point within the Sierra Madre mountain chain, was nonetheless a main transportation, communication, and economic route within Mesoamerica.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bodies_of_water">Bodies of water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bodies of water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yojoa_Lake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yojoa_Lake.jpg/220px-Yojoa_Lake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yojoa_Lake.jpg/330px-Yojoa_Lake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yojoa_Lake.jpg/440px-Yojoa_Lake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yojoa_Lake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lake_Yojoa" title="Lake Yojoa">Yojoa Lake</a> in Honduras.</div></div></div>
<p>Outside of the northern Maya lowlands, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> are common throughout Mesoamerica. Some of the more important ones served as loci of human occupation in the area. The longest river in Mesoamerica is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Usumacinta" title="Usumacinta">Usumacinta</a>, which forms in Guatemala at the convergence of the <a href="/wiki/Salinas_River_(Guatemala)" title="Salinas River (Guatemala)">Salinas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chixoy_River" title="Chixoy River">Chixoy</a> and La <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasion_River" title="Pasion River">Pasion River</a> and runs north for 970 km (600 mi)—480 km (300 mi) of which are navigable—eventually draining into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. Other rivers of note include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_de_Santiago" title="Rio Grande de Santiago">Rio Grande de Santiago</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grijalva_River" title="Grijalva River">Grijalva River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Motagua_River" title="Motagua River">Motagua River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ul%C3%BAa_River" title="Ulúa River">Ulúa River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hondo_River_(Belize)" title="Hondo River (Belize)">Hondo River</a>. The northern Maya lowlands, especially the northern portion of the Yucatán peninsula, are notable for their nearly complete lack of rivers (largely due to the absolute lack of topographic variation). Additionally, no lakes exist in the northern peninsula. The main source of water in this area is <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a> that are accessed through natural surface openings called <a href="/wiki/Cenote" title="Cenote">cenotes</a>.
</p><p>With an area of 8,264 km<sup>2</sup> (3,191 sq mi), <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nicaragua" title="Lake Nicaragua">Lake Nicaragua</a> is the largest lake in Mesoamerica. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chapala" title="Lake Chapala">Lake Chapala</a> is Mexico's largest freshwater lake, but <a href="/wiki/Lake_Texcoco" title="Lake Texcoco">Lake Texcoco</a> is perhaps most well known as the location upon which <a href="/wiki/Tenochtitlan" title="Tenochtitlan">Tenochtitlan</a>, capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> Empire, was founded. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pet%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1" title="Lake Petén Itzá">Lake Petén Itzá</a>, in northern Guatemala, is notable as where the last independent Maya city, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tayasal" title="Tayasal">Tayasal</a> (or Noh Petén), held out against the Spanish until 1697. Other large lakes include <a href="/wiki/Lake_Atitl%C3%A1n" title="Lake Atitlán">Lake Atitlán</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Izabal" title="Lake Izabal">Lake Izabal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_El_Salvador" title="Geography of El Salvador">Lake Güija</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemoa" title="Lemoa">Lemoa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Managua" title="Lake Managua">Lake Managua</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Almost all <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> are present in Mesoamerica; the more well known are the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_Barrier_Reef_System" title="Mesoamerican Barrier Reef System">Mesoamerican Barrier Reef System</a>, the second largest in the world, and <a href="/wiki/Mosquito_Coast" title="Mosquito Coast">La Mosquitia</a> (consisting of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rio_Platano_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Rio Platano Biosphere Reserve">Rio Platano Biosphere Reserve</a>, Tawahka Asangni, <a href="/wiki/Patuca_National_Park" title="Patuca National Park">Patuca National Park</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosawas_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Bosawas Biosphere Reserve">Bosawas Biosphere Reserve</a>) a <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a> second in size in the Americas only to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazonas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The highlands present <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_coniferous_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical coniferous forests">mixed</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">coniferous</a> forest. The biodiversity is among the richest in the world, though the number of species in the red list of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> grows every year.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology_and_culture">Chronology and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chronology and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Mesoamerican chronology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2472" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg/220px-Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg/330px-Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg/440px-Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a> is one of the largest archaeological sites, urban centers, and tourist attractions of the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a>. It is located in the archaeological region of the <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Basin" title="Petén Basin">Petén Basin</a> in what is now northern <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The history of human occupation in Mesoamerica is divided into stages or periods. These are known, with slight variation depending on region, as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleo-Indian" title="Paleo-Indian">Paleo-Indian</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology#Archaic_Era" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Archaic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology#Preclassic_Era" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Preclassic</a> (or Formative), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classic_Period" title="Classic Period">Classic</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postclassic_period" title="Postclassic period">Postclassic</a>. The last three periods, representing the core of Mesoamerican cultural fluorescence, are further divided into two or three sub-phases. Most of the time following the arrival of the Spanish in the 16th century is classified as the Colonial period.
</p><p>The differentiation of early periods (i.e., up through the end of the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology#Preclassic_Era" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Late Preclassic</a>) generally reflects <a href="/wiki/Sociocultural_evolution" title="Sociocultural evolution">different configurations of socio-cultural organization</a> that are characterized by <a href="/wiki/Urgesellschaft" title="Urgesellschaft">increasing socio-political complexity</a>, the adoption of new and different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_subsistence_techniques" title="List of subsistence techniques">subsistence strategies</a>, and changes in economic organization (including increased interregional interaction). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classic_Period" title="Classic Period">Classic</a> period through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postclassic_period" title="Postclassic period">Postclassic</a> are differentiated by the cyclical crystallization and fragmentation of the various political entities throughout Mesoamerica.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleo-Indian">Paleo-Indian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Paleo-Indian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Mesoamerican Paleo-Indian period precedes the advent of agriculture and is characterized by a nomadic <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunting and gathering</a> subsistence strategy. Big-game hunting, similar to that seen in contemporaneous <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, was a large component of the subsistence strategy of the Mesoamerican Paleo-Indian.  These sites had <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> blades and <a href="/wiki/Clovis_point" title="Clovis point">Clovis</a>-style fluted <a href="/wiki/Projectile_point" title="Projectile point">projectile points</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaic">Archaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Archaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Archaic period (8000–2000 BCE) is characterized by the rise of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mesoamerica" title="Agriculture in Mesoamerica">incipient agriculture</a> in Mesoamerica. The initial phases of the Archaic involved the cultivation of wild plants, transitioning into informal domestication and culminating with <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a> and agricultural production by the close of the period. Transformations of natural environments have been a common feature at least since the mid Holocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franco_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-franco-16">[16]</a></sup> Archaic sites include <i><a href="/wiki/Sipacate" title="Sipacate">Sipacate</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Escuintla" title="Escuintla">Escuintla</a>, Guatemala, where maize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pollen_analysis" title="Pollen analysis">pollen samples</a> date to c. 3500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roush_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-roush-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Preclassic.2FFormative"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Preclassic/Formative">Preclassic/Formative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Preclassic/Formative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Olmec_influences_on_Mesoamerican_cultures" title="Olmec influences on Mesoamerican cultures">Olmec influences on Mesoamerican cultures</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg/220px-San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg/330px-San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg/440px-San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Lorenzo_Monument_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Olmec_colossal_heads" title="Olmec colossal heads">Olmec Colossal Head</a> No. 3 1200–900 BCE</div></div></div>
<p>The first complex civilization to develop in Mesoamerica was that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a>, who inhabited the gulf coast region of <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a> throughout the Preclassic period. The main sites of the Olmec include <a href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo_Tenochtitl%C3%A1n" title="San Lorenzo Tenochtitlán">San Lorenzo Tenochtitlán</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Venta" title="La Venta">La Venta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tres_Zapotes" title="Tres Zapotes">Tres Zapotes</a>. Specific dates vary, but these sites were occupied from roughly 1200 to 400 BCE. Remains of other early cultures interacting with the Olmec have been found at <a href="/wiki/Takalik_Abaj" title="Takalik Abaj">Takalik Abaj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Izapa" title="Izapa">Izapa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Teopantecuanitlan" title="Teopantecuanitlan">Teopantecuanitlan</a>, and as far south as in <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Research in the Pacific Lowlands of Chiapas and Guatemala suggest that <a href="/wiki/Izapa" title="Izapa">Izapa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Monte_Alto_culture" title="Monte Alto culture">Monte Alto Culture</a> may have preceded the Olmec. <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon samples</a> associated with various sculptures found at the Late Preclassic site of <a href="/wiki/Izapa" title="Izapa">Izapa</a> suggest a date of between 1800 and 1500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Middle and Late Preclassic period, the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a> developed in the southern Maya highlands and lowlands, and at a few sites in the northern Maya lowlands. The earliest Maya sites coalesced after 1000 BCE, and include <a href="/wiki/Nakbe" title="Nakbe">Nakbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Mirador" title="El Mirador">El Mirador</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cerros" title="Cerros">Cerros</a>. Middle to Late <a href="/wiki/Preclassic_Maya" title="Preclassic Maya">Preclassic Maya</a> sites include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaminaljuy%C3%BA" title="Kaminaljuyú">Kaminaljuyú</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cival" title="Cival">Cival</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edzn%C3%A1" title="Edzná">Edzná</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cob%C3%A1" title="Cobá">Cobá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamanai" title="Lamanai">Lamanai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Komchen" title="Komchen">Komchen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dzibilchaltun" title="Dzibilchaltun">Dzibilchaltun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/San_Bartolo_(Maya_site)" title="San Bartolo (Maya site)">San Bartolo</a>, among others.
</p><p>The Preclassic in the central Mexican highlands is represented by such sites as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tlapacoya_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Tlapacoya (Mesoamerican site)">Tlapacoya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tlatilco" title="Tlatilco">Tlatilco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuicuilco" title="Cuicuilco">Cuicuilco</a>. These sites were eventually superseded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teotihuac%C3%A1n" title="Teotihuacán">Teotihuacán</a>, an important Classic-era site that eventually dominated economic and interaction spheres throughout Mesoamerica. The settlement of Teotihuacan is dated to the later portion of the Late Preclassic, or roughly 50 CE.
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valley_of_Oaxaca" title="Valley of Oaxaca">Valley of Oaxaca</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9_Mogote" title="San José Mogote">San José Mogote</a> represents one of the oldest permanent agricultural villages in the area, and one of the first to use pottery. During the Early and Middle Preclassic, the site developed some of the earliest examples of defensive <a href="/wiki/Palisade" title="Palisade">palisades</a>, ceremonial structures, the use of <a href="/wiki/Adobe" title="Adobe">adobe</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zapotec_writing" title="Zapotec writing">hieroglyphic writing</a>. Also of importance, the site was one of the first to demonstrate <a href="/wiki/Ascribed_status" title="Ascribed status">inherited status</a>, signifying a radical shift in socio-cultural and political structure. San José Mogote was eventual overtaken by <a href="/wiki/Monte_Alb%C3%A1n" title="Monte Albán">Monte Albán</a>, the subsequent capital of the <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilization" title="Zapotec civilization">Zapotec empire</a>, during the Late Preclassic.
</p><p>The Preclassic in western Mexico, in the states of <a href="/wiki/Nayarit" title="Nayarit">Nayarit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jalisco" title="Jalisco">Jalisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colima" title="Colima">Colima</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michoac%C3%A1n" title="Michoacán">Michoacán</a> also known as the Occidente, is poorly understood. This period is best represented by the thousands of figurines recovered by looters and ascribed to the "<a href="/wiki/Western_Mexico_shaft_tomb_tradition" title="Western Mexico shaft tomb tradition">shaft tomb tradition</a>".
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic">Classic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Classic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Classic">Early Classic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Early Classic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2434" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg/220px-Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg/330px-Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg/440px-Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piramide_de_la_Luna_072006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_the_Moon" title="Pyramid of the Moon">Pyramid of the Moon</a> viewed from atop of the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_the_Sun" title="Pyramid of the Sun">Pyramid of the Sun</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Classic period is marked by the rise and dominance of several polities. The traditional distinction between the Early and Late Classic are marked by their changing fortune and their ability to maintain regional primacy. Of paramount importance are Teotihuacán in central Mexico and <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a> in Guatemala; the Early Classic's temporal limits generally correlate to the main periods of these sites. Monte Albán in Oaxaca is another Classic-period polity that expanded and flourished during this period, but the Zapotec capital exerted less interregional influence than the other two sites.
</p><p>During the Early Classic, Teotihuacan participated in and perhaps dominated a far-reaching macro-regional interaction network. Architectural and artifact styles (talud-tablero, tripod slab-footed ceramic vessels) epitomized at Teotihuacan were mimicked and adopted at many distant settlements. <a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica#Sources" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">Pachuca</a> obsidian, whose trade and distribution is argued to have been economically controlled by Teotihuacan, is found throughout Mesoamerica.
</p><p>Tikal came to dominate much of the southern Maya lowlands politically, economically, and militarily during the Early Classic. An exchange network centered at Tikal distributed a variety of goods and commodities throughout southeast Mesoamerica, such as obsidian imported from central Mexico (e.g., Pachuca) and highland Guatemala (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica#Sources" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">El Chayal</a>, which was predominantly used by the Maya during the Early Classic), and <a href="/wiki/Jade_use_in_Mesoamerica" title="Jade use in Mesoamerica">jade</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Motagua_River" title="Motagua River">Motagua valley</a> in Guatemala. Tikal was often in conflict with other polities in the <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Basin" title="Petén Basin">Petén Basin</a>, as well as with others outside of it, including <a href="/wiki/Uaxactun" title="Uaxactun">Uaxactun</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caracol,_Belize" title="Caracol, Belize">Caracol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dos_Pilas" title="Dos Pilas">Dos Pilas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naranjo" title="Naranjo">Naranjo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calakmul" title="Calakmul">Calakmul</a>. Towards the end of the Early Classic, this conflict lead to Tikal's military defeat at the hands of Caracol in 562, and a period commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Tikal#Tikal_hiatus" title="Tikal">Tikal Hiatus</a>.
</p>

</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Classic">Late Classic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Late Classic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg/220px-Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg/330px-Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg/440px-Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xochicalco_Serpiente_Emplumada_GR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Xochicalco" title="Xochicalco">Xochicalco</a>, Temple of the Feathered Serpent, 650–900 CE</div></div></div>
<p>The Late Classic period (beginning c. 600 CE until 909 CE) is characterized as a period of interregional competition and factionalization among the numerous regional polities in the Maya area. This largely resulted from the decrease in Tikal's socio-political and economic power at the beginning of the period. It was therefore during this time that other sites rose to regional prominence and were able to exert greater interregional influence, including Caracol, <a href="/wiki/Cop%C3%A1n" title="Copán">Copán</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">Palenque</a>, and Calakmul (which was allied with Caracol and may have assisted in the defeat of Tikal), and <a href="/wiki/Dos_Pilas" title="Dos Pilas">Dos Pilas</a> <a href="/wiki/Aguateca" title="Aguateca">Aguateca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cancu%C3%A9n" title="Cancuén">Cancuén</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petexbat%C3%BAn" title="Petexbatún">Petexbatún</a> region of Guatemala. Around 710, Tikal arose again and started to build strong alliances and defeat its worst enemies. In the Maya area, the Late Classic ended with the so-called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_collapse" title="Maya collapse">Maya collapse</a>", a transitional period coupling the general depopulation of the southern lowlands and development and florescence of centers in the northern lowlands.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Classic_gallery">Late Classic gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Late Classic gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminal_Classic">Terminal Classic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Terminal Classic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0077_Uxmal.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/0077_Uxmal.JPG/220px-0077_Uxmal.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/0077_Uxmal.JPG/330px-0077_Uxmal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/0077_Uxmal.JPG/440px-0077_Uxmal.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0077_Uxmal.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the Nunnery Quadrangle at <a href="/wiki/Uxmal" title="Uxmal">Uxmal</a>, 10th century</div></div></div>
<p>Generally applied to the Maya area, the Terminal Classic roughly spans the time between c. 800/850 and c. 1000 AD. Overall, it generally correlates with the rise to prominence of <a href="/wiki/Puuc" title="Puuc">Puuc</a> settlements in the northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_Lowlands" title="Maya Lowlands">Maya lowlands</a>, so named after the hills where they are mainly found. Puuc settlements are specifically associated with a unique architectural style (the "Puuc architectural style") that represents a technological departure from previous construction techniques. Major Puuc sites include <a href="/wiki/Uxmal" title="Uxmal">Uxmal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sayil" title="Sayil">Sayil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labna" title="Labna">Labna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabah_(Maya_site)" title="Kabah (Maya site)">Kabah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxkintok" title="Oxkintok">Oxkintok</a>. While generally concentrated within the area in and around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puuc_hills" title="Puuc hills">Puuc hills</a>, the style has been documented as far away as at <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a> to the east and <a href="/wiki/Edzna" title="Edzna">Edzna</a> to the south.
</p><p>Chichén Itzá was originally thought to have been a Postclassic site in the northern Maya lowlands. Research over the past few decades has established that it was first settled during the Early/Late Classic transition but rose to prominence during the Terminal Classic and Early Postclassic. During its apogee, this widely known site economically and politically dominated the northern lowlands. Its participation in the circum-peninsular exchange route, possible through its port site of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Isla_Cerritos&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Isla Cerritos (page does not exist)">Isla Cerritos</a>, allowed Chichén Itzá to remain highly connected to areas such as central Mexico and <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>. The apparent "Mexicanization" of architecture at Chichén Itzá led past researchers to believe that Chichén Itzá existed under the control of a Toltec empire. Chronological data refutes this early interpretation, and it is now known that Chichén Itzá predated the Toltec; Mexican architectural styles are now used as an indicator of strong economic and ideological ties between the two regions.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postclassic">Postclassic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Postclassic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Postclassic (beginning 900–1000 CE, depending on area) is, like the Late Classic, characterized by the cyclical crystallization and fragmentation of various polities. The main Maya centers were located in the northern lowlands. Following Chichén Itzá, whose political structure collapsed during the Early Postclassic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayap%C3%A1n" title="Mayapán">Mayapán</a> rose to prominence during the Middle Postclassic and dominated the north for c. 200 years. After Mayapán's fragmentation, political structure in the northern lowlands revolved around large towns or city-states, such as <a href="/wiki/Oxkutzcab" title="Oxkutzcab">Oxkutzcab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ti%27ho" title="Ti'ho">Ti’ho</a> (<a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9rida,_Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Mérida, Yucatán">Mérida, Yucatán</a>), that competed with one another.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399068" data-file-width="589568" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg/330px-Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg/495px-Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg/660px-Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mesoam%C3%A9rica_y_Centroamerica_prehispanica_siglo_XVI.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mesoamerica and Central America in the 16th century before Spanish arrival<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (August 2020)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">Toniná</a>, in the Chiapas highlands, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaminaljuy%C3%BA" title="Kaminaljuyú">Kaminaljuyú</a> in the central Guatemala highlands, were important southern highland Maya centers. The latter site, Kaminaljuyú, is one of the longest occupied sites in Mesoamerica and was continuously inhabited from c. 800 BCE to around 1200 CE. Other important highland Maya groups include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" title="K'iche' people">K'iche'</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utatl%C3%A1n" title="Utatlán">Utatlán</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mam_people" title="Mam people">Mam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zaculeu" title="Zaculeu">Zaculeu</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Poqomam_language" title="Poqomam language">Poqomam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mixco_Viejo" title="Mixco Viejo">Mixco Viejo</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_people" title="Kaqchikel people">Kaqchikel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Iximche" title="Iximche">Iximche</a> in the Guatemalan highlands. The <a href="/wiki/Pipil_people" title="Pipil people">Pipil</a> resided in <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%27orti%27_people" title="Ch'orti' people">Ch'orti'</a> were in eastern Guatemala and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>.
</p><p>In central Mexico, the early portion of the Postclassic correlates with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Toltec" title="Toltec">Toltec</a> and an empire based at their capital, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tula,_Hidalgo" title="Tula, Hidalgo">Tula</a> (also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tollan" title="Tollan">Tollan</a>). <a href="/wiki/Cholula_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Cholula (Mesoamerican site)">Cholula</a>, initially an important Early Classic center contemporaneous with Teotihuacan, maintained its political structure (it did not collapse) and continued to function as a regionally important center during the Postclassic. The latter portion of the Postclassic is generally associated with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Mexica" title="Mexica">Mexica</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aztec_Empire" title="Aztec Empire">Aztec Empire</a>. One of the more commonly known cultural groups in Mesoamerica, the Aztec politically dominated nearly all of central Mexico, the Gulf Coast, Mexico's southern Pacific Coast (Chiapas and into Guatemala), Oaxaca, and <a href="/wiki/Guerrero" title="Guerrero">Guerrero</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarascan_state" title="Tarascan state">Tarascans</a> (also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pur%C3%A9pecha_people" title="Purépecha people">P'urhépecha</a>) were located in <a href="/wiki/Michoac%C3%A1n" title="Michoacán">Michoacán</a> and Guerrero. With their capital at <a href="/wiki/Tzintzuntzan_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Tzintzuntzan (Mesoamerican site)">Tzintzuntzan</a>, the Tarascan state was one of the few to actively and continuously resist Aztec domination during the Late Postclassic. Other important Postclassic cultures in Mesoamerica include the <a href="/wiki/Totonac" title="Totonac">Totonac</a> along the eastern coast (in the modern-day states of <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">Puebla</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hidalgo_(Mexico)" title="Hidalgo (Mexico)">Hidalgo</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Huastec_people" title="Huastec people">Huastec</a> resided north of the Totonac, mainly in the modern-day states of <a href="/wiki/Tamaulipas" title="Tamaulipas">Tamaulipas</a> and northern Veracruz. The <a href="/wiki/Mixtec" title="Mixtec">Mixtec</a> and Zapotec cultures, centered at <a href="/wiki/Mitla" title="Mitla">Mitla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaachila" title="Zaachila">Zaachila</a> respectively, inhabited Oaxaca.
</p><p>The Postclassic ends with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Mexico" title="Spanish conquest of Mexico">arrival of the Spanish</a> and their subsequent conquest of the Aztec between 1519 and 1521. Many other cultural groups did not acquiesce until later. For example, Maya groups in the Petén area, including the <a href="/wiki/Itza_people" title="Itza people">Itza</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tayasal" title="Tayasal">Tayasal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kowoj" title="Kowoj">Kowoj</a> at <a href="/wiki/Zacpeten" title="Zacpeten">Zacpeten</a>, remained independent until 1697.
</p><p>Some Mesoamerican cultures never achieved dominant status or left impressive archaeological remains but are nevertheless noteworthy. These include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otomi_people" title="Otomi people">Otomi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixe%E2%80%93Zoquean_languages" title="Mixe–Zoquean languages">Mixe–Zoque</a> groups (which may or may not have been related to the Olmecs), the northern <a href="/wiki/Uto-Aztecan_languages" title="Uto-Aztecan languages">Uto-Aztecan</a> groups, often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Chichimeca" title="Chichimeca">Chichimeca</a>, that include the <a href="/wiki/Cora_people" title="Cora people">Cora</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huichol_people" title="Huichol people">Huichol</a>, the Chontales, the Huaves, and the Pipil, Xincan and Lencan peoples of Central America.
</p>
