<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_characteristics">Other characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsistence">Subsistence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Subsistence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mesoamerica" title="Agriculture in Mesoamerica">Agriculture in Mesoamerica</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_diet_and_subsistence" title="Maya diet and subsistence">Maya diet and subsistence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zea_mays.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2670" data-file-width="1794" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Zea_mays.jpg/170px-Zea_mays.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Zea_mays.jpg/255px-Zea_mays.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Zea_mays.jpg/340px-Zea_mays.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zea_mays.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Examples of the diversity of <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aztec_Empire.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="13333" data-file-width="20000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Aztec_Empire.png/330px-Aztec_Empire.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Aztec_Empire.png/495px-Aztec_Empire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Aztec_Empire.png/660px-Aztec_Empire.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aztec_Empire.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Aztec Empire in 1512</div></div></div>
<p>By roughly 6000 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> living in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highland_(geography)" title="Highland (geography)">highlands</a> and lowlands of Mesoamerica began to develop agricultural practices with early cultivation of squash and chilli. The earliest example of <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> dates to c. 4000 BCE and comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guil%C3%A1_Naquitz" title="Guilá Naquitz">Guilá Naquitz</a>, a cave in Oaxaca. Earlier maize samples have been documented at the Los Ladrones cave site in <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, c. 5500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Slightly thereafter, semi-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrarian_community" title="Agrarian community">agrarian communities</a> began to cultivate other crops throughout Mesoamerica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Maize was the most common domesticate, but the common bean, tepary bean, scarlet runner bean, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jicama" title="Jicama">jicama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a> and squash all became common cultivates by 3500 BCE. At the same time, these communities exploited <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yucca" title="Yucca">yucca</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agave" title="Agave">agave</a> for fibers and <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a> materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> By 2000 BCE, corn was the staple crop in the region, and remained so through modern times. The Ramón or <a href="/wiki/Brosimum_alicastrum" title="Brosimum alicastrum">Breadnut tree</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Brosimum_alicastrum" title="Brosimum alicastrum">Brosimum alicastrum</a></i>) was an occasional substitute for maize in producing flour. Fruit was also important in the daily diet of Mesoamerican cultures. Some of the main ones consumed include <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papaya" title="Papaya">papaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guava" title="Guava">guava</a>, <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mamey_(disambiguation)" title="Mamey (disambiguation)">mamey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sapote" title="Sapote">zapote</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Annona" title="Annona">annona</a>.
</p><p>Mesoamerica lacked animals suitable for domestication, most notably domesticated large <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>. The lack of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draft_animals" title="Draft animals">draft animals</a> for transportation is one notable difference between Mesoamerica and the cultures of the South American Andes. Other animals, including the <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">duck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wild_turkey" title="Wild turkey">turkey</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a>. Turkey was the first to be domesticated locally, around 3500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dog_meat" title="Dog meat">Dogs</a> were the primary source of animal protein in ancient Mesoamerica,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and dog bones are common in midden deposits throughout the region.
</p><p>Societies of this region did hunt certain wild species for food. These animals included deer, <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbit</a>, birds, and various types of insects. They also hunted for luxury items, such as feline fur and bird plumage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mesoamerican cultures that lived in the lowlands and coastal plains settled down in agrarian communities somewhat later than did highland cultures due to the fact that there was a greater abundance of fruits and animals in these areas, which made a hunter-gatherer lifestyle more attractive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Fishing also was a major provider of food to lowland and coastal Mesoamericans creating a further disincentive to settle down in permanent communities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_organization">Political organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Political organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="269" decoding="async" height="358" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg/170px-ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg/255px-ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChanBahlumCatherwood.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%27inich_Kan_B%27alam_II" title="K'inich Kan B'alam II">K'inich Kan B'alam II</a>, the Classic period ruler of <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">Palenque</a>, as depicted on a <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ceremonial centers were the nuclei of Mesoamerican settlements. The temples provided spatial orientation, which was imparted to the surrounding town. The cities with their commercial and religious centers were always political entities, somewhat similar to the European <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a>, and each person could identify with the city where they lived.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Ceremonial centers were always built to be visible. Pyramids were meant to stand out from the rest of the city, to represent the gods and their powers. Another characteristic feature of the ceremonial centers is historic layers. All the ceremonial edifices were built in various phases, one on top of the other, to the point that what we now see is usually the last stage of construction. Ultimately, the ceremonial centers were the architectural translation of the identity of each city, as represented by the veneration of their gods and masters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stela" title="Stela">Stelae</a> were common public monuments throughout Mesoamerica, and served to commemorate notable successes, events and dates associated with the rulers and nobility of the various sites.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Trade_in_Maya_civilization" title="Trade in Maya civilization">Trade in Maya civilization</a></div>
<p>Given that Mesoamerica was broken into numerous and diverse ecological niches, none of the societies that inhabited the area were self-sufficient, although very long-distance trade was common only for very rare goods, or luxury materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> For this reason, from the last centuries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_the_Americas" title="Archaic period in the Americas">Archaic</a> period (8000 BC– 1000 BC) onward, regions compensated for the environmental inadequacies by specializing in the extraction of certain abundant natural resources and then trading them for necessary unavailable resources through established commercial trade networks.
</p><p>The following is a list of some of the specialized resources traded from the various Mesoamerican sub-regions and environmental contexts:
</p>
<ul><li>Pacific lowlands: <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cochineal" title="Cochineal">cochineal</a></li>
<li>Maya lowlands and the Gulf Coast: <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cacao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaguar" title="Jaguar">jaguar</a> skins, birds and bird feathers (especially <a href="/wiki/Quetzal" title="Quetzal">quetzal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macaw" title="Macaw">macaw</a>)</li>
<li>Central Mexico: Obsidian (<a href="/wiki/Pachuca" title="Pachuca">Pachuca</a>)</li>
<li>Guatemalan highlands: Obsidian (<a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica#Sources" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">San Martin Jilotepeque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica#Sources" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">El Chayal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica#Sources" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">Ixtepeque</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Motagua_River" title="Motagua River">Motagua River</a> valley</li>
<li>Coastal areas: <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a>, dry <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_shell" title="Animal shell">shell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_architecture" title="Mesoamerican architecture">Mesoamerican architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1729" data-file-width="2631" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_Yarumela.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration that recreates the structures of the archaeological site of <a href="/wiki/Yarumela" title="Yarumela">Yarumela</a> or El Chircal in Honduras, this place reflects the Olmec influence that existed in Central America in the pre-classic period.</div></div></div>
<p>Mesoamerican architecture is the collective name given to urban, ceremonial and public structures built by <a href="/wiki/List_of_pre-Columbian_cultures" title="List of pre-Columbian cultures">pre-Columbian civilizations</a> in Mesoamerica. Although very different in styles, all kinds of mesoamerican architecture show some kind of interrelation, due to very significant cultural exchanges occurred during thousands of years. Among the most well-known structures in Mesoamerica, the flat-top <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_pyramids" title="Mesoamerican pyramids">pyramids</a> are a landmark feature of the most developed urban centers.
</p><p>Two characteristics are most notable in Mesoamerican architecture. Firstly, the intimate connection between geography, astronomy and architecture: very often, urban centers or even single buildings are aligned to cardinal directions and/or along particular constellations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Secondly, iconography was considered integral part of architecture, with buildings often being adorned with images of religious and cultural significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calendrical_systems">Calendrical systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Calendrical systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_calendars" title="Mesoamerican calendars">Mesoamerican calendars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maya_days.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="682" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/Maya_days.jpg/220px-Maya_days.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/Maya_days.jpg/330px-Maya_days.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/Maya_days.jpg/440px-Maya_days.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maya_days.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Head Variant" or "Patron Gods" glyphs for Maya days</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tikalemblem.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="224" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Tikalemblem.jpg/220px-Tikalemblem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Tikalemblem.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tikalemblem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Maya_script" title="Maya script">emblem glyph</a> of Tikal (Mutal)</div></div></div>
<p>Agriculturally based people historically divide the year into four seasons. These included the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solstices" title="Solstices">solstices</a> and the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equinoxes" title="Equinoxes">equinoxes</a>, which could be thought of as the four "directional pillars" that support the year. These four times of the year were, and still are, important as they indicate seasonal changes that directly impact the lives of Mesoamerican agriculturalists.
</p><p>The Maya closely observed and duly recorded the seasonal markers. They prepared almanacs recording past and recent solar and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipses" title="Lunar eclipses">lunar eclipses</a>, the phases of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, the periods of <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, the movements of various other planets, and conjunctions of celestial bodies. These almanacs also made future predictions concerning celestial events. These tables are remarkably accurate, given the technology available, and indicate a significant level of knowledge among Maya <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the many types of calendars the Maya maintained, the most important include a 260-day cycle, a 360-day cycle or 'year', a 365-day cycle or year, a lunar cycle, and a Venus cycle, which tracked the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_period" title="Synodic period">synodic</a> period of Venus. Maya of the European contact period said that knowing the past aided in both understanding the present and predicting the future (Diego de Landa). The 260-day cycle was a calendar to govern agriculture, observe religious holidays, mark the movements of celestial bodies and memorialize public officials. The 260-day cycle was also used for divination, and (like the Catholic calendar of saints) to name newborns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The names given to the days, months, and years in the Mesoamerican calendar came, for the most part, from animals, flowers, heavenly bodies, and cultural concepts that held symbolic significance in Mesoamerican culture. This calendar was used throughout the history of Mesoamerican by nearly every culture. Even today, several Maya groups in Guatemala, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" title="K'iche' people">K'iche'</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Q%27eqchi%27_people" title="Q'eqchi' people">Q'eqchi'</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_people" title="Kaqchikel people">Kaqchikel</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mixe_people" title="Mixe people">Mixe people</a> of Oaxaca continue using modernized forms of the Mesoamerican calendar.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_systems">Writing systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Writing systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_writing_systems" title="Mesoamerican writing systems">Mesoamerican writing systems</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Mojarra_Estela_1_(Escritura_superior).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg/170px-La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg/255px-La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg/340px-La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Mojarra_Estela_1_(Escritura_superior).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the earliest examples of the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_writing_systems" title="Mesoamerican writing systems">Mesoamerican writing systems</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epi-Olmec_script" title="Epi-Olmec script">Epi-Olmec script</a> on the <a href="/wiki/La_Mojarra_Stela_1" title="La Mojarra Stela 1">La Mojarra Stela 1</a> dated to around 150 CE. Mesoamerica is one of the five places in the world where <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a> has developed independently.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mesoamerican scripts deciphered to date are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logosyllabary" title="Logosyllabary">logosyllabic</a> combining the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logograms" title="Logograms">logograms</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a>, and they are often called <a href="/wiki/Hieroglyph" title="Hieroglyph">hieroglyphic</a> scripts. Five or six different scripts have been documented in Mesoamerica, but archaeological dating methods, and a certain degree of self-interest, create difficulties in establishing priority and thus the forebear from which the others developed. The best documented and deciphered Mesoamerican writing system, and therefore the most widely known, is the classic <a href="/wiki/Maya_script" title="Maya script">Maya script</a>. Others include the <a href="/wiki/Olmec_hieroglyphs" title="Olmec hieroglyphs">Olmec</a>, Zapotec, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epi-Olmec_script" title="Epi-Olmec script">Epi-Olmec/Isthmian</a> writing systems. An extensive <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_literature" title="Mesoamerican literature">Mesoamerican literature</a> has been conserved partly in indigenous scripts and partly in the postinvasion transcriptions into <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>.
</p><p>The other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glyph_(archaeology)" title="Glyph (archaeology)">glyphic</a> writing systems of Mesoamerica, and their interpretation, have been subject to much debate. One important ongoing discussion regards whether non-Maya Mesoamerican texts can be considered examples of true writing or whether non-Maya Mesoamerican texts are best understood as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictographic" title="Pictographic">pictographic</a> conventions that express ideas, specifically religious ones, but don't represent the phonetics of spoken language.
</p><p>Mesoamerican writing is found in several mediums, including large stone monuments such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stelae" title="Stelae">stelae</a>, carved directly onto architecture, carved or painted over stucco (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murals" title="Murals">murals</a>), and on <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>. No Precolumbian Mesoamerican society is known to have had widespread literacy, and literacy was probably restricted to particular social classes, including scribes, painters, merchants, and the nobility.
</p><p>The Mesoamerican book was typically written with brush and colored inks on a paper prepared from the inner bark of the ficus amacus. The book consisted of a long strip of the prepared bark, which was folded like a screenfold to define individual pages. The pages were often covered and protected by elaborately carved book boards. Some books were composed of square pages while others were composed of rectangular pages.
</p><p>Following the Spanish conquests in the sixteenth century, Spanish friars taught indigenous scribes to write their languages in alphabetic texts. Many oral histories of the prehispanic period were subsequently recorded in alphabetic texts.  The indigenous in central and southern Mexico continued to produce written texts in the colonial period, many with pictorial elements.  An important scholarly reference work is the <i><a href="/wiki/Handbook_of_Middle_American_Indians" title="Handbook of Middle American Indians">Handbook of Middle American Indians</a>, Guide to Ethnohistorical Sources</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_codices" title="Mesoamerican codices">Mesoamerican codices</a> survive from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec_codices" title="Aztec codices">Aztec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_codices" title="Maya codices">Maya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixtec_codices" title="Mixtec codices">Mixtec</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilization" title="Zapotec civilization">Zapotec</a> regions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic">Arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Maya_numerals" title="Maya numerals">Maya numerals</a></div>
<p>Mesoamerican <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a> treated <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">numbers</a> as having both literal and symbolic value, the result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualistic_cosmology" title="Dualistic cosmology">dualistic</a> nature that characterized Mesoamerican ideology. As mentioned, the Mesoamerican numbering system was vigesimal (i.e., based on the number 20).
</p><p>In representing numbers, a series of bars and dots were employed. Dots had a value of one, and bars had a value of five. This type of arithmetic was combined with a symbolic numerology: '2' was related to origins, as all origins can be thought of as doubling; '3' was related to household fire; '4' was linked to the four corners of the universe; '5' expressed instability; '9' pertained to the underworld and the night; '13' was the number for light, '20' for abundance, and '400' for infinity. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/0_(number)" title="0 (number)">concept of zero</a> was also used, and its representation at the Late Preclassic occupation of <a href="/wiki/Tres_Zapotes" title="Tres Zapotes">Tres Zapotes</a> is one of the earliest uses of zero in human history.
</p>
<h3><span id="Food.2C_medicine.2C_and_science"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Food,_medicine,_and_science">Food, medicine, and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Food, medicine, and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Mesoamerica would deserve its place in the human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> if its inhabitants had only created <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, in terms of harvest weight the world's most important crop. But the inhabitants of Mexico and northern Central America also developed <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a>, now basic to <a href="/wiki/Italian_cuisine" title="Italian cuisine">Italian cuisine</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_peppers" title="Bell peppers">peppers</a>, essential to <a href="/wiki/Thai_cuisine" title="Thai cuisine">Thai</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_food" title="Indian food">Indian food</a>; all the world's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squashes</a> (except for a few domesticated in the United States); and many of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beans" title="Beans">beans</a> on dinner plates around the world. One writer estimated these indigenous tribes developed three-fifths of the crops now grown in cultivation, most of them in Mesoamerica. Having secured their food supply, the Mesoamerican societies turned to intellectual pursuits. In a millennium or less, a comparatively short time, they invented their own <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero" title="Zero">zero</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Maize played an important role in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_feasts" title="Mesoamerican feasts">Mesoamerican feasts</a> due to its symbolic meaning and abundance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Gods were praised and named after.
</p><p>Companion planting was practiced in various forms by the indigenous peoples of the Americas. They domesticated <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita_pepo" title="Cucurbita pepo">squash</a> 8,000 to 10,000 years ago, then <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Phaseolus_vulgaris" title="Phaseolus vulgaris">common beans</a>, forming the <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">Three Sisters</a> agricultural technique. The cornstalk served as a <a href="/wiki/Trellis_(architecture)" title="Trellis (architecture)">trellis</a> for the beans to climb, and the beans <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">fixed nitrogen</a>, benefitting the maize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fray <a href="/wiki/Bernardino_de_Sahag%C3%BAn" title="Bernardino de Sahagún">Bernardino de Sahagún</a> collected extensive information on plants, animals, soil types, among other matters from native informants in Book 11, The Earthly Things, of the twelve-volume <i>General History of the Things of New Spain,</i> known as the <a href="/wiki/Florentine_Codex" title="Florentine Codex">Florentine Codex</a>, compiled in the third quarter of the sixteenth century. Bernardino de Sahagún reported the ritualistic use of <a href="/wiki/Psilocybe" title="Psilocybe">Psilocybe</a> mushrooms known to the <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Psilocybin_mushroom" title="Psilocybin mushroom">teōnanācatl</a></i> (agglutinative form of <i>teōtl</i> (god, sacred) and <i>nanācatl</i> (mushroom) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahuatl_language" title="Nahuatl language">Náhuatl</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> An earlier work, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badianus_Manuscript" title="Badianus Manuscript">Badianus Manuscript</a> or <a href="/wiki/Libellus_de_Medicinalibus_Indorum_Herbis" title="Libellus de Medicinalibus Indorum Herbis">Libellus de Medicinalibus Indorum Herbis</a> is another Aztec codex with written text and illustrations collected from the indigenous viewpoint. The <a href="/wiki/Aztec_use_of_entheogens" title="Aztec use of entheogens">ancient Aztecs used a variety of entheogens</a> within their society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence shows that wild animals were captured and traded for symbolic and ritual purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology_and_worldview">Mythology and worldview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mythology and worldview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_religion" title="Mesoamerican religion">Mesoamerican religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_creation_myths" title="Mesoamerican creation myths">Mesoamerican creation myths</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_world_tree" title="Mesoamerican world tree">Mesoamerican world tree</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aztec_religion" title="Aztec religion">Aztec religion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec_mythology" title="Olmec mythology">Olmec mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_religion" title="Maya religion">Maya religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maya_mythology" title="Maya mythology">Maya mythology</a></div><p> Shared traits in Mesoamerican mythology are characterized by their common basis as a religion that—though in many Mesoamerican groups developed into complex polytheistic religious systems—retained some shamanistic elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg/220px-Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg/330px-Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg/440px-Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mexico.Xoloitzcuintle.01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_Hairless_Dog" title="Mexican Hairless Dog">xoloitzcuintle</a> is one of the <a href="/wiki/Nagual" title="Nagual">naguales</a> of the god <a href="/wiki/Quetzalcoatl" title="Quetzalcoatl">Quetzalcoatl</a>. In this form, it helps the dead cross the Chicnahuapan, a river that separates the world of the living from the dead.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg/170px-Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg/255px-Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg/340px-Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mascara_Dios_Murcielago.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zapotec mask of the Bat God.</div></div></div>
<p>The great breadth of the Mesoamerican <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deities" title="Deities">deities</a> is due to the incorporation of ideological and religious elements from the first primitive religion of Fire, Earth, Water and Nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Astral divinities (the sun, stars, constellations, and Venus) were adopted and represented in anthropomorphic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoomorphic" title="Zoomorphic">zoomorphic</a>, and anthropozoomorphic sculptures, and in day-to-day objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The qualities of these gods and their attributes changed with the passage of time and with cultural influences from other Mesoamerican groups. The gods are at once three: creator, preserver, and destroyer, and at the same time just one. An important characteristic of Mesoamerican religion was the dualism among the divine entities. The gods represented the confrontation between opposite poles: the positive, exemplified by light, the masculine, force, war, the sun, etc.; and the negative, exemplified by darkness, the feminine, repose, peace, the moon, etc.
</p><p>The typical Mesoamerican cosmology sees the world as separated into a day world watched by the sun and a night world watched by the moon. More importantly, the three superposed levels of the world are united by a <a href="/wiki/Ceiba" title="Ceiba">Ceiba</a> tree (<i>Yaxche'</i> in Mayan). The geographic vision is also tied to the cardinal points. Certain geographical features are linked to different parts of this cosmovision. Thus mountains and tall trees connect the middle and upper worlds; caves connect the middle and nether worlds.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacrifice">Sacrifice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Sacrifice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Generally, sacrifice can be divided into two types: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloodletting_rituals_in_Maya_culture" title="Bloodletting rituals in Maya culture">autosacrifice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The different forms of sacrifice are reflected in the imagery used to evoke ideological structure and sociocultural organization in Mesoamerica. In the Maya area, for example, stele depict bloodletting rituals performed by ruling elites, eagles and jaguars devouring human hearts, jade circles or necklaces that represented hearts, and plants and flowers that symbolized both nature and the blood that provided life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Imagery also showed pleas for rain or pleas for blood, with the same intention to replenish the divine energy. Ritual sacrifice was done in efforts to appease the gods, and was done with the purpose of protection of the population.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Autosacrifice">Autosacrifice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Autosacrifice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting_in_Mesoamerica" title="Bloodletting in Mesoamerica">Bloodletting in Mesoamerica</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice_in_Maya_culture" title="Sacrifice in Maya culture">Sacrifice in Maya culture</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_sacrifice_(Codex_Laud,_f.8).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="658" data-file-width="682" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Human_sacrifice_%28Codex_Laud%2C_f.8%29.png/220px-Human_sacrifice_%28Codex_Laud%2C_f.8%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Human_sacrifice_%28Codex_Laud%2C_f.8%29.png/330px-Human_sacrifice_%28Codex_Laud%2C_f.8%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Human_sacrifice_%28Codex_Laud%2C_f.8%29.png/440px-Human_sacrifice_%28Codex_Laud%2C_f.8%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_sacrifice_(Codex_Laud,_f.8).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ritual human sacrifice portrayed in <a href="/wiki/Codex_Laud" title="Codex Laud">Codex Laud</a></div></div></div>
<p>Autosacrifice, also called <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting" title="Bloodletting">bloodletting</a>, is the ritualized practice of drawing blood from oneself. It is commonly seen or represented through iconography as performed by ruling elites in highly ritualized ceremonies, but it was easily practiced in mundane sociocultural contexts (i.e., non-elites could perform autosacrifice). The act was typically performed with <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> <a href="/wiki/Prismatic_blade" title="Prismatic blade">prismatic blades</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stingray_injury#Biological_structure_of_the_barb" title="Stingray injury">stingray spines</a>, and blood was drawn from piercing or cutting the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earlobe" title="Earlobe">earlobes</a>, and/or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitals" title="Genitals">genitals</a> (among other locations). Another form of autosacrifice was conducted by pulling a rope with attached thorns through the tongue or earlobes. The blood produced was then collected on <a href="/wiki/Amate" title="Amate">amate</a> held in a bowl.
</p><p>Autosacrifice was not limited to male rulers, as their female counterparts often performed these ritualized activities. They are typically shown performing the rope and thorns technique. A recently discovered queen's tomb in the Classic Maya site of <a href="/wiki/El_Per%C3%BA_(Maya_site)" title="El Perú (Maya site)">Waka</a> (also known as El Perú) had a ceremonial stingray spine placed in her genital area, suggesting that women also performed bloodletting in their genitalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_sacrifice">Human sacrifice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Human sacrifice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice_in_Aztec_culture" title="Human sacrifice in Aztec culture">Human sacrifice in Aztec culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice_in_Maya_culture" title="Human sacrifice in Maya culture">Human sacrifice in Maya culture</a></div>
<p>Sacrifice had great importance in the social and religious aspects of Mesoamerican culture. First, it showed death transformed into the divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Death is the consequence of a human sacrifice, but it is not the end; it is but the continuation of the cosmic cycle. Death creates life—divine energy is liberated through death and returns to the gods, who are then able to create more life. Secondly, it justifies war, since the most valuable sacrifices are obtained through conflict. The death of the warrior is the greatest sacrifice and gives the gods the energy to go about their daily activities, such as the bringing of rain. Warfare and capturing prisoners became a method of social advancement and a religious cause. Finally, it justifies the control of power by the two ruling classes, the priests and the warriors. The priests controlled the religious ideology, and the warriors supplied the sacrifices. Historically it was also in discussion that those sacrificed were chosen by the gods, this idea of being "chosen" was decided by the gods. This was then displayed by acts, such as being struck by lightning. If someone was struck by lightning and a sacrifice was needed they would often be chosen by their population, as they believed they were chosen by the gods.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ballgame">Ballgame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Ballgame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballgame" title="Mesoamerican ballgame">Mesoamerican ballgame</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg/220px-Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg/330px-Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg/440px-Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tikal_central_ballcourt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A small ceremonial ballcourt at <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ballgamemarker.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1079" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Ballgamemarker.jpg/220px-Ballgamemarker.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Ballgamemarker.jpg/330px-Ballgamemarker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Ballgamemarker.jpg/440px-Ballgamemarker.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ballgamemarker.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballgame" title="Mesoamerican ballgame">Ballgame marker</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">classic</a> <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Lowland Maya</a> site of <a href="/wiki/Chinkultic" title="Chinkultic">Chinkultic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> depicting a ballplayer in full gear</div></div></div>
<p>The Mesoamerican ballgame was a sport with ritual associations played for over 3000 years by nearly all pre-Columbian peoples of Mesoamerica. The sport had different versions in different places during the millennia, and a modern version of the game, <a href="/wiki/Ulama_(game)" title="Ulama (game)">ulama</a>, is still played in a few places.
</p><p>Over 1300 <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballcourt" title="Mesoamerican ballcourt">ballcourts</a> have been found throughout Mesoamerica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> They vary considerably in size, but they all feature long narrow alleys with side-walls to bounce the balls against.
</p><p>The rules of the ballgame are not known, but it was probably similar to volleyball, where the object is to keep the ball in play. In the most well-known version of the game, the players struck the ball with their hips, though some versions used forearms or employed rackets, bats, or handstones. The ball was made of solid rubber, and weighed up to 4 kg or more, with sizes that differed greatly over time or according to the version played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFilloy_Nadal200130_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFilloy_Nadal200130-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeyenaar2001125–26_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeyenaar2001125–26-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the game was played casually for simple recreation, including by children and perhaps even women, the game also had important ritual aspects, and major formal ballgames were held as ritual events, often featuring human sacrifice.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mesoamerican <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> included a broad understanding of the cycles of planets and other celestial bodies. Special importance was given to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> as the morning and evening star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Observatories were built at some sites, including the round observatory at <a href="/wiki/Seibal" title="Seibal">Ceibal</a> and the "Observatorio" at <a href="/wiki/Xochicalco" title="Xochicalco">Xochicalco</a>. Often, the architectural organization of Mesoamerican sites was based on precise calculations derived from astronomical observations. Well-known examples of these include the <a href="/wiki/El_Castillo,_Chichen_Itza" title="El Castillo, Chichen Itza">El Castillo</a> pyramid at Chichen Itza and the Observatorio at <a href="/wiki/Xochicalco" title="Xochicalco">Xochicalco</a>. A unique and common architectural complex found among many Mesoamerican sites are <a href="/wiki/E-Group" title="E-Group">E-Groups</a>, which are aligned so as to serve as astronomical observatories. The name of this complex is based on <a href="/wiki/Uaxactun" title="Uaxactun">Uaxactun</a>'s "Group E", the first known observatory in the Maya area. Perhaps the earliest observatory documented in Mesoamerica is that of the <a href="/wiki/Monte_Alto_culture" title="Monte Alto culture">Monte Alto culture</a>. This complex consisted of three plain stelae and a temple oriented with respect to the <a href="/wiki/Pleiades" title="Pleiades">Pleiades</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism_of_space_and_time">Symbolism of space and time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Symbolism of space and time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mexico0047.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Mexico0047.jpg/220px-Mexico0047.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Mexico0047.jpg/330px-Mexico0047.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Mexico0047.jpg/440px-Mexico0047.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mexico0047.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Teotihuacan#Site_layout" title="Teotihuacan">Avenue of the Dead</a> in <a href="/wiki/Teotihuacan" title="Teotihuacan">Teotihuacan</a>, an example of a Mesoamerican settlement planned according to concepts of directionality</div></div></div>
<p>It has been argued that among Mesoamerican societies the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> are associated with the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_points" title="Cardinal points">cardinal compass points</a> and linked together by the <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Dates or events were always tied to a compass direction, and the calendar specified the symbolic geographical characteristic peculiar to that period. Resulting from the significance held by the cardinal directions, many Mesoamerican architectural features, if not entire settlements, were planned and oriented with respect to directionality.
</p><p>In Maya cosmology, each cardinal point was assigned a specific color and a specific jaguar deity (<a href="/wiki/Maya_mythology#Bacabs" title="Maya mythology">Bacab</a>). They are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hacha-kyum" title="Hacha-kyum">Hobnil</a></b>, Bacab of the <a href="/wiki/East" title="East">East</a>, associated with the color red and the <i>Kan</i> years</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/List_of_Maya_gods_and_supernatural_beings#C" title="List of Maya gods and supernatural beings">Can Tzicnal</a></b>, Bacab of the <a href="/wiki/North" title="North">North</a>, assigned the color white and the <i>Muluc</i> years</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/List_of_Maya_gods_and_supernatural_beings#Z" title="List of Maya gods and supernatural beings">Zac Cimi</a></b>, Bacab of the <a href="/wiki/West" title="West">West</a>, associated with the color black and the <i>Ix</i> years</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hacha-kyum" title="Hacha-kyum">Hozanek</a></b>, Bacab of the <a href="/wiki/South" title="South">South</a>, associated with the color yellow and the <i>Cauac</i> years.</li></ul>
<p>Later cultures such as the <a href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_people" title="Kaqchikel people">Kaqchikel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" title="K'iche' people">K'iche'</a> maintain the association of cardinal directions with each color, but utilized different names.
</p><p>Among the Aztec, the name of each day was associated with a cardinal point (thus conferring symbolic significance), and each cardinal direction was associated with a group of symbols. Below are the symbols and concepts associated with each direction:
</p>
<ul><li><b>East</b>: <a href="/wiki/American_crocodile" title="American crocodile">croco</a> <a href="/wiki/Morelet%27s_crocodile" title="Morelet's crocodile">dile</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpent_(symbolism)" title="Serpent (symbolism)">serpent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, cane, and movement. The East was linked to the world priests and associated with vegetative fertility, or, in other words, tropical exuberance.</li>
<li><b>North</b>: wind, death, the dog, the jaguar, and <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Chert" title="Chert">chert</a>). The north contrasts with the east in that it is conceptualized as dry, cold, and oppressive. It is considered the nocturnal part of the universe and includes the dwellings of the dead. The dog (<a href="/wiki/Xoloitzcuintle" title="Xoloitzcuintle">xoloitzcuintle</a>) has a very specific meaning, as it accompanies the deceased during the trip to the lands of the dead and helps them cross the river of death that leads into nothingness. (<i>See also</i> <a href="/wiki/Dogs_in_Mesoamerican_folklore_and_myth" title="Dogs in Mesoamerican folklore and myth">Dogs in Mesoamerican folklore and myth</a>).</li>
<li><b>West</b>: the house, the deer, the <a href="/wiki/New_World_monkey" title="New World monkey">monkey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>, and rain. The west was associated with the cycles of vegetation, specifically the temperate high plains that experience light rains and the change of seasons.</li>
<li><b>South</b>: rabbit, the <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizard</a>, dried herbs, the <a href="/wiki/New_World_vulture" title="New World vulture">buzzard</a>, and flowers. It is related on the one hand to the luminous Sun and the noon heat, and on the other with rain filled with alcoholic drink. The rabbit, the principal symbol of the west, was associated with farmers and with <a href="/wiki/Pulque" title="Pulque">pulque</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_and_religious_art">Political and religious art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Political and religious art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Mesoamerican_art" title="Category:Mesoamerican art">Category:Mesoamerican art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="810" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg/220px-Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg/330px-Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg/440px-Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tzompantli_Tovar.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Art with ideological and political meaning: depiction of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tzompantli" title="Tzompantli">tzompantli</a></i> (skull-rack) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramirez_Codex" title="Ramirez Codex">Ramirez Codex</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mesoamerican <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">artistic</a> expression was conditioned by <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> and generally focused on themes of <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> and/or sociopolitical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_(sociology)" title="Power (sociology)">power</a>. This is largely based on the fact that most works that survived the Spanish conquest were public monuments. These monuments were typically erected by rulers who sought to visually legitimize their sociocultural and political position; by doing so, they intertwined their lineage, personal attributes and achievements, and legacy with religious concepts. As such, these monuments were specifically designed for public display and took many forms, including <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a>, architectural <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a>, and other types of architectural elements (e.g., roofcombs). Other themes expressed include tracking time, glorifying the city, and veneration of the gods—all of which were tied to explicitly aggrandizing the abilities and the reign of the ruler who commissioned the artwork.
</p><p>The majority of artwork created during this historical time was in relation to these topics, religion and politics. Rulers were drawn and sculpted. Historical tales and events were then translated into pieces of art, and art was used to relay religious and political messages.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerica&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maya_music" title="Maya music">Maya music</a></div><p>Archaeological studies have never discovered any written music from the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian era</a>, but musical instruments were found, as well as carvings and depictions, that clearly show how music played a central role in the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayan</a> religious and societal structures, for example, as accompaniment to celebrations and funerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Some Mesoamerican civilizations, such as the Maya, commonly played various instruments such as drums, flutes and whistles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Although most of the original Mayan music disappeared following the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonization</a>, some of it mixed with the incoming Spanish music and exists to date.</p><div style="clear:both;"></div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em">
